RATING: 8.5
Set collection with bluffing and multiple paths to victory.

Game is divided into 12 rounds (provinces) which are determined randomly.  You visit each province sequentially, and on your turn, choose to play a card(s) or withdraw.  You always get cards at the end of your turn, but the last player gets one less card, and if you outright withdraw you get one extra card.  There are multiple ways to score: winning a palace placement, and thus a character chit (2 chits equate to a special powered card), items that are available within the province upon placements (ie: goods, +2 points, free cards), palace connection points (which are cumulative), and the elephant tile (which contains goods that are cumulative).  Also you are awarded points for the largest consecutive run of cards at the end of the game.

In order to have any power in this game, you must have cards, but cardplay must be of the same colour, and you can also combine these with the coveted white cards.  There is a significant bluffing element as well, as the other player's don't really know what you have or how much you're willing to play.  It should be noted that all cards played (except the special whites) are lost, win or lose.  It pays to be the last man standing, as you will often be the big winner of characters, goods, etc.  

Overall an outstanding game that is highly strategic, and yet not overly heavy.  Masterful in that it's a card-based game that is skill, rather than luck driven.  Scales very well for 3-5 players, despite rumours to the contrary.

RATING: 7.5
This is very much a first impression, but I feel positive about the game so far.  Boy is there a lot going on.  I hadn't really got a clue what I was doing for the first 3 or 4 turns, but started to get a grip on it after that...well at least enough to finish second and improve my scoring dramatically.  Since it's a quick game and pretty brain-burning, it's hard to get fully to grips with on first play.There is a lot going on here and yet most of it whilst vaguely familiar seems utterly new.  There are elements of poker, elements of chicken (maybe a few ideas that Iliad has since borrowed), elements of building long links (not exactly TtR, but kind of along those lines).  Despite that, I didn't feel that any of those similarities are very strong and the game is really very different form other games I've played.  I like it, but how much I like it remains to be seen.

RATING: 5
Played this with 3 players and we all enjoyed it. The cards could have been better designed - too plain looking and we kept getting confused with the special cards and the oval marker pictures at the bottom, which were often mistaken for the real thing when played. The little oval markers are too small and two of the markers look so similar (we call them bagpipe guy and pants dude). The scoring mechanism is better than T&E and Samurai because it just makes more sense and the majority of points are distributed with each province visit, meaning that there won't be surprising point gains at the end of the game.

UPDATE: May consider ditching this one too, it's good but the design is really distracting. It'll go to the top of my wish list if it gets reissued. The Knizia name may be enough to make it a keeper. UPDATE 2: Too drab and plain ugly to keep - yes, a game has got to look half-way decent for me, otherwise I won't care to introduce it to others. Getting rid of this, but will repurchase as a redesigned game.

RATING: 5
decide what you want to do, talk about it, note who is ahead of what, pick a temple spot, pick cards, trade out special cards, count your chains, etc.  when i look back after a game, i find that most of the play time was spent on everyone doing simple accounting.  the sheer volume of it presents an illusion of depth, but the only decisions i feel like i ultimately make are what to go after, and how far i'm willing to go if i meet resistance.  everything can be distilled down to that level of simplicity, but i care about how much time i spend between decisions - or at least how entertained i am between them.  i like the tension of what's underneath if you strip away the distractions, but then what do you have over something like beowulf or condottiere?  i can dig those, especially the former.

RATING: 9
[b]Deutscher Spiele Preis 2000[/b]

[i]Posted: 3/30/2009, Rated: 9[/i]
{3P: ???, 4P: 10, 5P: 9}

The [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgamedesigner/2]good Doctor[/url] has designed so many games, that it's difficult to keep track of them all.  With such megahits as [i][thing=42]Tigris & Euphrates[/thing][/i] and [i][thing=12]Ra[/thing][/i], [i]Taj Mahal[/i] had slipped through the cracks of my consciousness.  Ironically, I had picked up a copy over three years ago as part of the [family=58]Alea Big Box series[/family].  Only after a concerted effort to explore this game did it finally make it to the table.

Was I ever wrong to wait.  [i]Taj Mahal[/i] belongs in the pantheon of Knizia greats.

The auction mechanism is both agonizing and efficient.  On top of that core mechanic, the game is about "chain scoring:" linking your provinces together, or collecting commodities.  With four players, there is a perfect balance between the ability to extend an auction at the risk of losing premium temple placement within a province.

Recognizing potential chains then leads to hand management, so that you have the necessary cards to compete in those provinces.  The long term strategy merges beautifully with the tactical considerations of each auction.

[i]Taj Mahal[/i] is an amazing game.  I'm tempted to rate it a "10" out of the gate, but I don't want to be too hasty before trying it with three, and with five players.


[i]Updated 4/3/12, Rated: 9[/i]

I finally had the chance to play this game with five.  It was a learning/re-introduction game, so we weren't playing anywhere near optimally.  The one thing that stands out is that the auction can be brutal if you have that many players: if someone chooses to go long with you on a particular province, you can be left with nothing after having spent a lot of cards.  I think the risk/reward ratio is better with four players, which is why I'm rating the 5p game a "9" while the 4p game is a "10."  Overall score: still a "9."

RATING: 8.5
After a long time of having mainly new-ish titles as the main course on our game nights, Tadsch Mahal came back onto the table. When you get caught up in all the excitement about the latest bling, it can be easy to forget exactly how enjoyable some gems of the past were. Tadsch Mahal had slipped so far down memory lane that most of us needed a serious rule reminder before play commenced. And it does say something about its graceful aging that just over an hour later we were all caught up in mutual enthusiasm, not too dissimilar from when we played it first time round. Auntie Alea is still looking good.

This is probably more a board-driven card-game than vice-versa. Handmanagement is the name of the game. The board mainly dictates the order in which sets and bonus events will become available. It also adds another way to increase your overall score to standard set collecting, by stringing regions together with effective palace placements. You're gonna have to pay close attention during all 12 rounds, and the most important moment happens right at the start: figuring out your rough strategy for this set-up, and what the crucial provinces are for which you'll have to be ready. This, and roughly a dozen plan-B's.

We're blessed to have a group in which all player's are capable of winning in all sorts of ingenious ways, and everyone will do their bit to stop a runaway leader from emerging. That does certainly adds to the joy that can be had with this game. Not only does it offer a variety of plausible strategies, including some for the more -ahem- [i]enterprising[/i] amongst us, it also means that no player is likely to benefit disproportionately from someone else's bad moves. With the right crowd it will inevitably lead to memorable and tightly fought contests. But on the flip side of that same coin, it is also likely to be a spoiler when the makeup of a group is more diverse.

There is one masterstroke in this game, and it's how the auctions are resolved. It's not the player who ultimately ends up with the largest majority in a colour, who will take the spoils, but the player who has a majority (or majorities) during their turn, and decides to cash in rather than keep fighting for further spoils. It is a subtle variation on trick taking that can make a few well-chosen cards utterly superior against players who think that simple card-hoarding, or even card-counting, will give them the edge in a decisive endgame.

A lot of people here call it, quite rightly, a game of chicken. Getting involved in a long fight and walking away with nothing will ruin your hand for a few turns, and will cost you dearly. Judging when to wimp out is a crucial survival skill. However, since you are also dealing with a table full of other chickens, it offers also an opportunity to feed others the right crumbs, the little bit of grains that they will settle for, yet still leave you taking the sweetest corn [i]without[/i] triggering that costly fight to obtain them[/i]. Very few games will give as many options to mess with your competitors' heads as this one. If you like headology and think that judging other people's motives, and predicting their actions, is a skill, then this is one hell of a master class. If that doesn't appeal to you then I guess this will just be a classy hell.

The most baffling thing, is that this game has stackloads of trackable information, yet somehow it plays quick, doesn't feel heavy, has barely any downtime, and even in a group in which people like to think their moves through, still clocks in at just over and hour in a 5-player game. Maybe it has simply too much trackable info, and people are relying more on gut-instincts.

I usually prefer games that are chockfull of agonizing dilemmas that only use a handful of elements to create them. Tadsch Mahal is certainly on the high end of ingredients, but, with [i]Eschnapur[/i], it is one of the few mechanism-rich bidding games I really like. When you add a lot to the fabric of a building, it only works if all elements add something unique, yet disappear into one coherent structure, that lets you discover something new each time you look closer. It is down to the skill of the architect if it can be pulled off. This is certainly a random-set-up set-collecting Knizia with the type of intricate hand-management that has to be seen from all angles, in glorious 3D. Last night was a great Polaroid, but for once, if you keep snapping, players will keep smiling, no matter how often you take them back for "one more", as if you put 'em in front of a whole new building. Chickens ripe for plucking.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
[b]Best with: 4[/b]


Comparing Rialto to Taj Mahal (from a geeklist comment I made):

It (Rialto) definitely ends up feeling quite different while playing but here are some similarities:

1. the obvious- the random setup in which areas are resolved in a different order each game. 

2. you "visit" a new region each round of card play- players vie for control of the region before moving on to the next one. 1+2 are sort of the same, but the spacial element feels similar. In Taj you're planning strategically which areas you are going to attempt to dominate before the game even starts- that's sort of present here as well.

3. Rather than the cards having all assets spread amongs the different suits and bidding for everything all in one big round- Rialto is more compartmentalized. Each "gift" is auctioned off separately with players bidding cards in suits that only win them that particular item. It would be like in Taj if the structure was "first we go around to decide who wins the elephants, then we'll go around for the green lady and so on...

4. (IN Rialto) The spacial considerations involving the networking of your assets is supplanted by a weaker, but still important, one-off goal of being the first person with your men in each of 3 regions. This is the closest analogue to the networking of Taj. 

Rialto is lighter, more focused on card drafting rather than bidding. It's absolutely different- depth, playtime, strat vs tactics- but after playing Rialto I couldn't help but think Taj was an influence- even if only at the most general level.

RATING: 8
Win with as little possible.Try to keep at least two to three sets of 3-5 cards of a single color and/or white cards. That way when you go three or four cards in and you still have nothing, you are not out for the next visit. You could at least withdraw and get some matches to use in the next visit.In fact, maybe all withdraws should be decided more on what can be gained from the supply and less on what can be gained from the visit. Something  to try at least.

[b]Getting Started[/b]
After the game is set up, deal 6 cards to each player. Randomly determine the starting player. That player will play first. Start in Province 1. Play is one colored card, or one white card and a colored card each turn, or withdraw. If withdraw, take prizes for having majority of leaders, elephants or grand viziers. Score each good and duplicates (dupes can score multiple times) and/or place palace on good then score each good and duplicates (same as above). After scoring draw two cards from face up supply. Repeat until all players have withdrawn. Last player takes last single card from face up supply. Draw cards to create new supply. Move on to next province. Next clockwise player goes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A pretty abstract game with an Indian theme, simple mechanics but quite deep gameplay. Except for each players' starting hand, all information is open. An experienced gamer could plot a plan to victory were it not for the bidding mechanics. 

The game is set collection and route connection through the auctioning of six different resources. One resource gives sets of goods, one resource gives a connection point and four other resources are used for both. Most cards in hand contain a combination of two resources to bid on and in turn someone plays a card or passes. Upon passing majority is checked, where ties deliver nothing. Add to this a few special cards to spice up strategic choices, and that's about the whole game.

I love how the little metallic Taj Mahal pieces interlock when stacking. It's nice having something to do during an AP fase of an opponent.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
1/18/05 - 2 plays. Commerce tiles are always placed at random except for the No. 12 (it is always placed in the province containing Agra) so the board setup is never the same twice. Cards are the driving force to gain influence and potentially earns you the right to place a palace in a province which may or may not lead you to score extra points for picking up goods or extra points chit and/or connecting provinces (1 point for every province which become connected to the current placement of a palace). This all translates into a few different means of achieving points and the victory. Based on the geeks rating system...this is a '10'...cannot forsee tiring of this game.

BRAND NEW! MINT!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Knizia is by far my favorite designer and I think this is his very best. 

The possibility of spending resources and gaining nothing creates more tension than any game I have ever played. Beyond that though, the hand management choices are subtle and brilliant. The fact that you need two chits to steal a special card, and how to time that, that long suit is worth end-game points, in addition to wanting to win goods and bonus chits and extend connections make the decisions multi-layered and difficult. You need a long-term plan, but there is no way you won't be changing that plan multiple times throughout the game. It is a perfect melding of strategy and tactics, wrapped in a series of vicious auctions. 

Taj Mahal is not a game that holds your hand. I have seen players blunder their way to dreadful showings, so it does require players who pay attention and are competent card players - so it is squarely a gamers game. Also I have become a convert to only wanting to play with 4. So the game is fragile. But under the right circumstances, this is my absolute favorite gaming experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Clever card / board game with lots of interesting mechanics and ways to score. Rather abstract but I liked the poker-like card play which balances the choice of folding early and taking the best cards to improve your future hands with the hope that you'll win the elephant and a number of the characters each with their special ability to gain points and special cards now. Probably not the sort of game to play with analytical types as this would slow it down and make it no fun at all. A solution to this might be to hand out VP chips which could be kept secret rather than using the scoring track. In all of my games to date there has been a runaway leader problem with no way of catching up.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
After about 10 plays, my gut tells me that this is Knizia's masterpiece.  The choices before you are so simple -- play, or withdraw -- but the considerations are vastly complex.  If you play, you've got to be sure you'll win, because usually you can't afford to part with valuable cards for nothing.  You need to manage and to carefully build your hand.  You need to plan ahead based on which regions you're bidding first, which ones you're last, and where the different resources are.  You've got to pay some attention to the hands your opponents are building.  You have to choose between the board spots with the bonus chips, and the ones that most help you to build chains. You have to know the advantages/disadvantages of going first/last.  And despite all the planning, no two games are the same.  It's a game in which you can't afford to gamble, and lose.  Elegant and unforgiving, like putting at Augusta when the greens are fast.  Everyone's in the same boat, but only one of you can manage things the best.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
good game but I struggle to win this one
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Rated 9 after one play. Wow... I was surprised how much I liked this game. I read the rules, played a few "dummy" rounds to learn the game, but the actual first play really grabbed me.

I've never shied away from direct player interaction, and this game has it in spades. The unique auction of committing cards and losing them whether you win an aspect of the auction or not generates a grand game of chicken. It's fun to push people around and try to muscle them with better cards. I can see the connection to poker. It's a card-driven game with a board for adding various means for scoring.

Who knows if my rating stays this high, but any game that offers this degree of interaction and angst in choices is normally going to sit very well with me.

Update after 2nd play with five players: I'm still loving this game, though a five player game is pretty rough. This game hits all my sweet spots--auction, direct interaction, tough choices and that sensation of wanting to do three things but having only the means to do two. Other players are a bit more lukewarm to this game than myself, but I'll continue to champion it for now.

Another update: I love this game, but I don't find other people really enjoying it as much as I do. It's a rough game for new players. Still, I love the auction mechanic and I'm willing to hold onto this for now to see if the game finds an audience.

Update: Well, traded this one away. I still like the game, but no one else in my local gaming group is clamoring to play this one. I'd gladly play again but I don't need to own this game without any audience for it.

RATING: 7
The sheer number of decisions and things to keep track of are impressive; and I like the core card playing aspect which encourages nasty games of chicken, the outcome (or avoidance) of which often determine the final winner. This game has 3 major paths to victory, and is fascinating in that one is best off choosing a strategic path from the start of the game, but some wiggle room is definitely necessary, and knowing when to pick your fights (and when to bow out) is critically important.

However, the game only plays at it's best (IMHO) with 4 players (3 allows auctions to go too cheaply, makes folding without playing a card to get that 3rd card to rebuild your hand almost unthinkable because of the smaller number of players, and makes linking too easy, while 5 makes linking nigh impossible, and thus makes the game overly elephant and princess card based). A solid game, but the best at 4 scale make it a very occasional play for me. I used to own this, but ultimately had to let it go because of this requirement. If I was rating this purely on the 4 scale it would pretty easily roll in at 8, but I think both 3 and 5 are lacklustre, and mean that I generally avoid playing this game, so I have to knock it down to 7 overall.

RATING: 5
Taj Mahal is basically 'Chicken' cast into a modern jacket. By playing cards with symbols on them you claim several objects to be won in an artifical-looking province in India, but you can only [i]get[/i] them if you still have the majority in those symbols once your turn comes again. By actually claiming or simply withdrawing you remove your cards from the table, which may cause someone else to obtain a majority automagically. This part of the game is rather unique; if I have seen it used elsewhere I cannot remember it, and at this stage I've seen a not-insignificant amount.

I like the tension this 'bidding' creates; there's both turn angst and player order chaos to consider. What I'm not sure about is how this mechanism is embedded in the rest of the game. The cards are exceedingly important to what you can do, and although you can decide to hoard cards and [i]then[/i] strike in whatever form, there doesn't seem to be a lot of variability in how you approach the strategy and tactics of this game. Other strategies are easy to spot and can also be blocked effectively: this doesn't mean that it always happens, but [i]if[/i] it happens the player following that strategy won't be happy. Add to this that some of the palaces you place get placed there through 'collateral bonuses', meaning you did not intend to win one, but actually won one without fighting for it. Loss of cards without winning anything for it [i]really[/i] bites individually, so most players are inclined to just give a palace to another player. This is probably some point balancing mechanism, but the haphazard and accidental way it functions is weird.

All in all there are some good things about Taj Mahal. In the past I really liked this game. But when it comes to the table nowadays I see a somewhat artifical game with too many rough little patches and spots to make it hold my attention for more than the single event. The fact that I never bring this to the table, preferring other titles instead, is an additional nail in the coffin. I've downgraded my rating as a consequence: the game simply lost a bit of its shine over time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.12346
My 2nd auction-based game after Monopoly. 

Though there's enough rules running in tandem to ensure multiple viable strategies, they were simple enough to be explained in a few minutes.

I felt I fully understood the game after a few rounds and given the absence of anything analogous to Puerto Rico's buildings, I'd soon seen everything being used and felt fully in control - it was now down to me to weigh up the options.

Once strategies are decided, the game moves fairly quickly and - for a change - I wasn't holding the group up.

Though my strategy won on this occasion, I can foresee the need to adapt to others' play.

Though the cards ostensibly add randomness, looking at those available and evaluating the need to back out early to select better cards becomes a greatly strategic element.

RATING: 9
I'll See Your Two Elephants and Raise You a Crown.
There are still a few Reiner games I have not tried yet, but so far I feel this is far and away his best. I almost feel this way after each of his new games but this one is still going strong after a full year of playing. It is one of the few games I do not get tired of after repeated playings.
I tried it two nights ago with 5 players for the first time. While I thought it was slightly broken with that many players, it was still a lot of fun. I found two problems with 5 players: One is that the Elephants are about the only way to win. The second problem that I found was the white card that gives 2 victory points when played was also too powerful. This is a four player game. Every turn is gutwrenching unless you've decided just to bow out and go for cards. The games do not last too long and each round everyone is watching what's going on so there is no real down time. It is a lot like poker except what you are losing instead of chips are the cards you are putting down and these are very valuable and hard to come by.
I have seen on the web that some think that it is too confusing and there are too many ways to score, but I play with an 11 year old girl and her mom and they have absolutely no problem with the game. I get the feeling that these people have just read the rules and decided it was too difficult. Once you start playing it all falls into place.

RATING: 10
Players are Indian nobility on a tour through 12 provinces.  That aside, this is a wonderful game of bidding and bluff for six "prizes" available in each province.  The prizes are claimed by having a majority in those prizes at the point your withdraw from the round - a stroke of genius that you have to experience to realize how clever it is.  Points are earned based on prizes won, via set collecting, forming chains of palaces on the board, or through bonuses via other means.  Tense and exciting, with every round a game of chicken waiting for someone to blink, yet if you pay attention there is surprisingly little luck.  One of the "must have" German games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
I consider Taj Mahal Knizia's magnum opus and prize it as the apex of great design.

In every one of the twelve round, there is an obvious incentive to play: to gain points and win!. But at times you must sit out an entire round in order to replenish resources. The very decisions of when to fight at all is often a big one in Taj Mahal.

Once rounds begin, there is a devilish system of bidding in different currencies to win the palaces. The conditions of the auction are cruel. No one is declared a winner of a palace until none of the other players are even tying their bid. So it is very difficult to win multiple palaces. Also others may tie your bids in order to exhaust your resources. A very confrontational game at heart! Wondering whether you can outlast your opponents in Taj Mahal is more tense than poker.

When you do use your turn to quit, you only get teh palaces you are undisputed leader in. And you immediately place palaces, and then another sub-game heppens. Becasue there are bonus points given for making chains! Finally, you immediately replenish your cards by drafting from the pool drawn before the round started. 

Because of the placement and drafting, you sometimes have an incentive to bid for only palace and then quickly quit. Especially to pick the best location and draft the best cards before they are gone! 

Learning to balance all of these countervailing costs and rewards is always a challange, even to veterans. Never does one always get what they want. Taj Mahal it is a constant game of comprimise, adaptation, and pushing-your-luck. 

You have to using rely on very little infomation about your opponents' resources and goals, and often play from the gut. It is a very rewarding and fun game, though grasping the goals can be difficult at first. 
 
If you can muddle through your introductary game you will discover that this is the enduring gem of Eurogames! I give my highest recommendation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Lot of stuff going on here, even or a heavy Knizia.  Played once, and I'd like to try it again.  My game was with 5 players, and I wonder if it would be better with 4 - making connections seemed to be underpowered vs. goods.

UPDATE March 2014
After a couple years of playing "modern" Euros, returning to Taj Mahal helps me appreciate it for the masterpiece it is.  This game takes you on a roller coaster where you feel alternatively powerful and near helpless, and riding out those waves is the key to success.  The design is razor sharp, but also allows for creativity and brutal player interaction.  Unlike a lot of the current crop of Eurogames, there is no rubber banding here.  Players will likely not all finish within a few points of each other.  If you mess up, you overextend, you miscalculate, you will be punished for it, and I miss that in my Eurogames!  This should all rights be in the top 50.

RATING: 4
This game completely breaks down if players don't all play it "the right way."

It's very easy for one player to get on track for a runaway victory. In theory, it's not all that hard for an opponent to put up a roadblock against such a victory, but in practice, you have to sacrifice your own progress completely -- making no progress to help yourself -- just to thwart the leader. So what results is a giant game of "chicken" where no one wants to be one to flinch and "take one for the team" to stop the leader. If no one stops the leader, he will win, but whoever DOES throw in to stop the leader guarantees that he himself won't win -- he'll just open the possibility for the second place player to sneak in and steal the victory.

Hard to believe Reiner Knizia's responsible for this.

RATING: 9
Really solid Knizia title (back when he was innovative and interesting, which doesn't seem to be the case anymore).  The card play is quite tense and you have to plan ahead for future provinces.  You have to gauge when to drop out and when to play a large group of cards, because getting little or nothing for a large card expenditure is going to hurt. Commodity scoring and palace scoring are the key methods for earning points, but palace chains require winning buildings in key locations to make it work.  I like the pace of the game, the depth of thinking about the cards, and the future planning involved.   Very engaging game, and it is competitive and intense - I like that.

RATING: 6.5
The bluffing/bidding mechanic is just plain fun, where you often dont know whether to pull out of a hand or to try to outlast everyone; nice tension and lots of available decisions to be made.  A few problems in the game, though: tile-pictures seem to score too much by their exponential growth, and so the luck factor can influence the game a bit too much, or leads to single-minded strategies (get elephants); and the game is unforgiving if in early rounds you bid too many cards and fall behind.  In too many games, 1 or 2 players pull out way ahead early (and stay there), making it dull and discouraging for trailing players, who have a hard time catching up.  Probably needs very experienced players who can guess opponents' likely moves to have a close game.

Nekem ez a játék a szimpatikusabbjai közül való, már elsőre tetszett - még annak ellenére is, h a megszerzett 14 pontommal nagyjából 30 ponttal lemaradva voltam utolsó, gyakorlatilag a játék során egyszer sem volt esélyem semmire. RATING: 7
Taj Mahal is a good game, but I feel that playing it for the first time 14 years after its release has made it a little less stunning to me than it might have a long time ago.  Even still, I was surprised to see that I was able to catch up at least to a battle for 3rd despite my terrible play of the opening two turns.

What I enjoyed most was the gambling aspect, which probably says more about me than the actual game.  Like poker, it's simply a matter of raising the bet each time you go around the table, but there's no option to call.  Coupled with the option to withdraw from the bidding at your leisure, which provides benefits if timed correctly, there's a really neat metagame layered on top of Taj Mahal as you guess and second-guess your opponents.

I'd like to see that applied to a different sort of game, but within Taj Mahal, it works just fine.

RATING: 6
I can really see why people say: if you like poker you should try Taj Mahal. Knowing when to fold from a visitation or to potentially bluff and proceed is an important aspect of the game. The card play and hand management is really the heart of the game but you have a bit of a spatial board element as well as a set collection for points to contend with. There are also special cards for the players to compete for. These are not only strong because they allow you add a bonus to a played card but they come back to you every round until another player steals the card from you. Being that I've only played it once, I'm not really sure how much I like it.

RATING: 10
Best design I've ever seen. You are trying to get points spatially, by set collection, by collecting bonuses, and by possession of the +2 card. A little bit of press-your-luck, a little bit of bluffing, and some hand management. You don't refill your hand by drawing cards blindly from a deck; you get to draw 2 cards (usually) from a set of face-up cards...but only at the point where you decide to stop competing for the round. Withdraw early and you get your choice. Withdraw late and you get what's left. Awesome awesome game, and about as medium in weight as Euros go.

RATING: 7
Each of Taj Mahal's individual mechanics is well implemented (and remniscent of those of many of Knizia's other titles) and the game, on paper, appears compelling and well balanced. However, in actual gameplay I found that the game to be less than the sum of its parts. Certainly the actions (or inaction) of other players and randomness had some bearing on the end result, but I was unpleasantly surprised that my off-balance "strategy" of ignoring goods and going exclusively after building chains of influence markers (often with Mogul) and holding the Princess card largely to the exclusion of the other tokens and goods gave me the win (and by the margin that it did). Perhaps I simply knew when to withdraw and my opponents did not, but I find that scenario unlikely. [Please note that these comments come after only a single play. I'd gladly give it another go.]

RATING: 9
[Hand Management, Set Collection, Network Connection, Political, India] {3P:7, 4P:9, 5P:8} Reinier Knizia takes his stomping grounds, the bidding genre, one step further with Taj Mahal. Cards are the main resource of the game, and you need to parley your cards into more resources, or directly into the Influence Points you need to win. As usual, multiple paths to victory and deep strategy combine with tense bidding tactics to produce a superior game. *** UPDATE: Still a nine, a fantastically frustrating but ultimately rewarding game which I always lose by a LOT but keep returning to because I can't figure it out. This may eventually challenge the realm of 10 despite my record of futility, because it's so uniquely maddening. Amazing, amazing game. *** UPDATE: What more can I say? Taj Mahal, welcome to the Pantheon of Godly Games. *** UPDATE: What is it about not being able to win that generates so much obsession? Well, I've won Taj now. Several times. And I've got a handle on it. It's tenuous, but with understanding comes clarity and closure. This remains an outstanding, Holy game, but it's not the Holy of Holies. I return Taj to demigod status, and there she will stay.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another fantastic auction game from Knizia. Taj Mahal is incredibly tight. The twist that you can't spend all of your available cards in one auction due to the colour restrictions leads to agonizing decisions every turn. If you miss the right opportunity to get out of an auctioning round things will get ugly, expensive, and you'll possibly have to leave with no gain at all. At the same time it's quite possible to win influence with mediocre hands. It all comes down to a question of timing and a little bluffing.

During our first game we thought the game might have a run-away leader issue, with one player having a 15 point lead at one point. By the end of the game, the top three players were within 3 points of each other, though, so it seems to be that some strategies simply pay off earlier than others. Outstanding!

RATING: 9
This is a rating likely to rise in time.  Taj Mahal is a unique game unlike any other I have played.  It's area control, but actually more commodity control.  Foresight is key in this one.  Knowing when to choose your battles (bids) is essential.

UPDATE:  I have been able to find time for this Knizia title recently and it has consequently jumped up a point and a half in my ratings.  I enjoy the poker element to this game as well as the tough decisions to be made between short term and long term goals.  The marriage of strategic and tactical play is seamless. I expect this might even rise further in the future.

UPDATE:  The risk management in this game balanced with an ever developing ebb and flow between tactics and strategy is amazing.  Each round offers new tension, new values for goods, and just mind melting goodness.  Essentially a bluff and bid game, Taj Mahal feels like so much more.  Managing your hand is essential, and the somewhat unintuitive nature of the game can sometimes create situations, in the heat of play, where emotions become more convincing than your wits  - and you can end up self destructing by not backing out of a lost cause.  Few games work on the level Taj does for me.  Taj successfully bores into the cranium and births dormant competitiveness on a more primal level.  I rarely win, but that's because greed and foolish play have blinded me too often.  This game is brilliant!

RATING: 7
A unique blend of mechanics.  Great press-your-luck card play that interacts very strongly with the need for good board positioning.  Also, the palace bits are way cool.

Unfortunately I've become convinced that "shrewd" play does not correlate very well with high scores.  Each player will have certain rounds in which it is extremely advantageous to earn specific rewards, because that player's palace chain could be extended or some key commodities could be obtained.  However, it is often not within the player's power to ensure a good outcome on these key hands--the remaining players' actions are too unpredictable to ensure success.  As a result, I believe that outcomes are mostly random; this has caused my rating to decrease.

RATING: 8
[b] Taj Mahal[/b] [i] Reiner Knizia 2006[/i]
Taj Mahal is a very solid game, easy to understand (way easier to score than most of Knizias games as well) and good for three, four or five players. Although its theme is a little bit pasted and dry it works well. The game is also short, 90/100 minutes for five experienced players. Comparing this gaming time with its difficulty and the strategic options that the game offers, the result is one of the best ratios available in the gaming industry among the short-medum difficult games, Some kingmaking can exist since a novice player can retreat his cards too soon or stay playing cards in a stuborn way when he must give up. The game is perfect for four players, but very playable with three or five.

RATING: 9
I enjoy Taj Mahal immensely, although the lack of control can be be frustrating at times. The game's best feature is that it supports a wide variety of paths to victory, both in terms of strategy and tactics.  Playing Taj Mahal is a very tense experience, with the feeling that you're walking a tightrope and could tumble with the slightest breeze.  I've raised my rating from 8 to 9 because it gets better and better the more you play it, which is to me the sign of a great game.

Unfortunately though, and unusually for a Knizia game, Taj Mahal is only at its best with exactly four players.  With three, there are simply too many items up for auction and not enough competition for those items.  With five, the game feels very random and/or arbitrary because there are too many players to act between your card plays.  You can't realistically predict what's going to happen in the auction, and so you spend more time hoping and less time planning.

Taj Mahal has often been compared to Poker, probably because like Poker, the key decision you're making again and again is whether to back down or to gamble more resources on the fight.  Both sometimes involve bluffing, and both nearly always require you to assess your opponent's strength and temperament.  But I don't care for Poker and I love Taj Mahal so the correlation is probably pretty weak.  Poker is a battle of psychology and probability stripped bare of almost all other game elements.  Taj Mahal has multiple goals, multiple paths to those goals, and multiple choices within those paths.  Each Taj Mahal "hand" is tightly woven into the previous hands and the following hands, while Poker hands are more like a series of unrelated events strung together.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Twenty-three plays. Win or lose, it's a blast, every time. Scores big in the game-as-a-depiction-of-life department, since you can look ahead and prepare for the future, yet you still must struggle at the decisive moment, and even then you can't really know how things are going to shake out...and that's not due to dumb luck or the turn of a card but rather odd chains of events that are fascinating to watch unfold, even if you wind up with the short end of the stick. Some have described it as a bluffing game, but I think it's more about discretion; the ability to convince others to drop out of fights is useful but knowing when to drop out yourself is a much more valuable skill.

RATING: 4
How well you do in Taj Mahal depends far more on what your opponents allow you to do, than what you choose to do yourself.

Careful, considered play allows you to position yourself to win conflicts. However, it's not only important to win, but to win quickly* and you have very little control over this.

(*Five conflicts occur simultaneously. As each is resolved, spoils and compensation are awarded from a common pool. "Win" a conflict late in the round and the pool may be so depleted that it would have been better to have lost earlier.)

RATING: 7
Taj Mahal is quintessentially "Poker: the board game" and achieves this quite wonderfully through a series of constraints. Namely: 
1. No hand holding: Taj Mahal provides a very limited set of cards for you to work with. You go overboard one hand? Get ready to suck it up in the next few ones as you lick your wounds. The game is all about the tension of doing the most with the least while others are trying to do the same.
2. Diverse yet limited scoring paths: Taj Mahal has two major pathways - set collection and palace connections. Both are viable, and provide their own sets of challenges and possibilities. Having only two means that they are choke points as well as paths: when 2 players go for each in a four player game, the game just lights up.
3. The in/out mechanic means that the stakes continue to rise, but not always as you'd expect them. There's the obvious card cost which is clearly spelled out: when a player matches your symbols, bailing means not getting anything. More cards are required. However, there is a more subtle cost: prizes also disappear as the hand progresses -- some might run with their winnings as soon as a marginal advantage presents itself in order to fight another day. The other cost of continuing things is the possibility of showing up too late for some items.

Overall, one of Knizia's better designs, but too its dismay doesn't flex very well to other-than-4 player counts.

RATING: 9
[Initial Impression: 8]
At the heart of Taj Mahal is a mind-bending auction, where each player can drop out on their turn, winning any items for which they currently have the highest bid.  Bids are made through card play, which is restricted to cards of a single color, so hand management is critical and you constantly have to agonize over whether to sit out an auction in order to build up your cards, risk a continued bid (using up even more scarce cards), or drop out to conserve your resources.  There are a few main routes for scoring, each of which requires implementing a long-range strategy, and the riskiness of each strategy depends entirely upon what strategies the other players pursue. This is a fascinating game, whose depth seems to grow with each play.

RATING: 7.5
This game doesn't appear to be anything more than another Knizia auction special at first, but...
The board has a very simple yet Indian flavour to it.  As the player markers accumulate, the colorful palaces are attractive, and the picture improves as the game builds out to it's end.

A very enjoyable game, not quite as streamlined as Ra, but perhaps slightly more thematic, and quite competitive.

RATING: 7
(04/2011) Comment based on 1 play.

Another Knizia area-control game, with cards. Like every other Knizia game, this is simple to learn, fast to play, yet has some subtle and complex decisions. I don't recall being too excited to play again, or to go buy it, but I'd play it again. Initial rating "7".

(05/26/2014) First time playing it in over three years. We all concluded that this is a very good game, and that we should bring it out more often! However, the card play, the symbols, and various funky rules reminded us of how difficult it is to play well, as well as remember how to play it. Play focuses around competition in the various provinces in India, where players try to build a string of castles and claim commodities. For each area, in turn order, each players play a card, with a number of symbol(s) dictating what category(s) they are competing for within each district, whether it is for the commodity in this area, or one of four personalities. Subsequent players can bow out, but if they are not leading in any of the five categories, will get nothing. Bowing out earlier allows a player to draw more cards from a common area, while bowing out later means fewer cards. If another player plays a card with a matching symbol, there is "competition" in that category, and the other player can play an additional card to increase the number of symbols. If no one else competes for a category, the player who has played the symbol will get the commodity of that city (scoring VP that is dependent on set collection) and/or a personality token. Two tokens of the same personality must be turned in, which gives the player a special "white" card that allows boosting of specific symbols during competition.

As described above, the game is somewhat difficult to describe, yet starts to make sense within a few rounds. Knizia is famous for making players think in unique and mathematically challenging ways. He is also famous for amply rewarding players that optimally play the "right amount". You may think you are winning because you played powerfully one moment, but Knizia has a way of penalizing you for overextending yourself in another. Taj Mahal is no different. Still, sensing what is the optimum is rather opaque--it is difficult to discern whether one is playing well or not. It turns out that the player who is not playing too aggressively nor too passively will win the game.

The game has excellent components, especially the castle pieces, which are made of high-quality plastic resin. Visually, the game is pleasing to watch unfold. The rules and mechanics take awhile to wrap one's head around, but become intuitive thereafter. I think this is a much better game than I thought three years ago. It is surely a "great" game, but it is rather understated, as it may take a number of plays to realize this. It is a solid "8".

(09/07/2014) Update after 3 plays. This game is getting a resurgence in our group, hitting the table more often, as it combines fascinating card play similar to Bridge with tense board position/network building.

RATING: 10
Mmm, tactical auction goodness from Knizia.  I must say Taj Mahal is one of the better designs by Reiner and an enjoyable, tight game that can be played in a reasonable amount of time - all good marks in my book.  Players can focus on short term profits, build up a late game scoring engine, play conservatively to accomplish what they need quickly turn to turn, or blow their wad all in one go.  There's lots of variety, depth, and elegance with all the charm you would come to expect from the the good doctor.  It's a fun and well done complex game without the heaviness typically associated.  In short, it's damn good.

Update:  This game is pure genius.  The level of complexity so innocently captured in the simple rules is really quite admirable.  With equally matched players, play can be absolutely brutal and devestating.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
20+ plays later I have yet to grow sick of this game, or even feel like I have come close to mastering it. I still can't properly pull off a Elephant strategy.

I like Taj Mahal for two mechanics: the chaining of palaces, and the "auction" to win the right to place palaces. Where to chain and why is a really interesting question because of the random set-up. You may have to play cards 3 times in a row to get the palaces you want, but that heavily depletes your hand. 

The main reason to play, however, is to participate in the battle for palaces. The entire "auction" is brilliant: the fact that you can only play cards of one color background, that you have to withdraw to win, and that ties garner neither player anything. I keep coming back for those battles.

Best with 4

RATING: 10
Set collection:hand management:bluffing:auctions:network connections

Original comments:  6 plays now and I'm very tempted to give this my first 10 rating as I find this fun unlike some people who may not have given this a fair chance, it will take anywhere from 1 to 3 games to fully grasp everything that is going on so be sure to give it that chance. More plays will confirm for me if it's high in replay which I think it is. All plays were with 4 players which would be the most balanced number for this game by far from what I can see, which is not to say it doesn't work with 3 or 5, just not quite as well... 

A STRATEGIC MASTERPIECE through and through and very unique and elegant as well. If you failed to see the elegance here then it is likely you have missed the beauty of the whole system, also if you think there is too much luck you should really play the game some more as this is one of Herr Doktors most luckless games. Lets also address the "Giant game of chicken" comments right now too, while there are some chicken wars to be had (which is one of my favourite parts of the game) calling the game a 'Giant game of chicken' is complete crap. Really, there is way more going on here than that and you might only get into one or maybe two "bidding wars" per game (1 or 2 rounds out of 12), at least if you know what you are doing but I guess I can see why those without a plan or who have little experience with this game might feel that way. 

Like T&E, it might take you 3 plays just to have a general clue on what the hell you should be doing (especially if you learn by yourself like I did), only then can you try working on all the multiple ways to score, some nice depth in this game but it is still quite accessible. There is so much to think about every single time you have to do something but not to the point of AP as long as you can retain a fair amount of details (actually at times you can feel yourself drowning in all the data floating around), decisions do get somewhat easier with more plays but alas always remain difficult to some degree providing great turn angst. If you really got some depth upstairs you can try adding 'counting cards' to the details to retain as to give yourself more of an edge...don't play this one for money with card sharks!

Poker influence (when to fold vs. push your luck and bluffing) along with set collection and network connection are all big parts of the game here. There is lots of planning through the whole game and these are long and short term plans. You also have to make tons of strong tactical decisions throughout like: when to fold first IF you can in order to place a palace where you want, do you fold first to get the cards that you need, do you fold first without playing to get an extra card and guarantee the cards that you want, do you want the resource from building or the VP chit, do you hold out to win the province influence, do you try to keep your paths connected, do you stop the other guy currently winning from connecting, and several more things to all come into play. On top of all these decisions, you need to perform the ultimate balancing act between cards, special cards, auctions, resources and network connections in order to beat good opponents. Another brilliant game design from the doctor and my fav from him so far. 

We knock this game off in 60 to 90 minutes, a perfect length for such a deep game, turns are also quite fast so your always involved (after enough plays) and every game is tense between good gamers. 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] The game is now maddening, I was loving it and now I'm hating it (in a good way) as I can't pull off a win and it has become quite the challenge, rating stays where it is however...GRRRRRRRRRRRRRR

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Rating edged up a tad more and ... YES! I finally juggled enough to win this game, what a ride I tell ya, between knowing (planning) when to have the occasional chicken war and just taking what you got and running it is one hell of a game!

[u]UPDATE:[/u] More FTF plays have confirmed this to be my Favourite Euro 4/06. I should note I am often a "gut" player, and this game really lends itself to that, so other gut players that like games with depth will likely take to this well

[u]FINAL UPDATE:[/u] After playing over 200 different types of games and playing this over 20 times I now feel confident enough to give this the one and only 10 rating I have for a Euro. This game virtually has it all with long and short term strategy, tactically rich, fun and high in replay (theme is weak however but forgivable). This is my untouchable and now that my original german copy got signed at Essen 06 it will never leave my house!

[u]Tidbit[/u] - The fan created 2 player variant is OUTSTANDING, sure wish I would have discovered it/tried it much sooner, one of my favourite 2 player games and I think a thing or two can be applied to the 3 player to tighten it up.

RATING: 8.75
Played for my first time in 2009 -- embarrassing, but I'm glad I did. Difficult decisions, delicious strategies, and anticipation of others' moves all meet in this quick to play and visually attractive game.

There was a slight learning hurdle for me to overcome, but by round 3 everything clicked. I won my second game of this--woo hoo!

This would have been a 9.5 for me if I had discovered this when it originally appeared. Even still, it does everything elegantly. Perhaps a bit too much luck in that sometimes things can just fall in your lap but at other times everything is a battle. The game's short play time 90% overcomes this element.

A little too chaotic with 5 players. Recommend 4.

Edit: Just played it again with 3 other vets and 1 new guy. There's definitely a one-game learning curve. I still think the game is an 8.75--just a little TOO much going on rules-wise/scoring-wise and just a little TOO chaotic, but TM still maintains its awesome elegance-strategy-intensity-duration factor. Highly recommended. (Ob. nitpick: Cards need a reprint--just one side (left, right? -- pick one!) only for the icons please--confusing to newbies and unnecessary to vets.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2015-08-30

Initial Rating: 6.0 (August 2015)

Nowadays, I expect almost nothing from Mr. Knizia´s games, so when I played Taj Mahal I wasn’t expecting much but I was pleasantly surprised. However this game was designed on 2000 so it seems I do like his earlier designs. 

Taj Mahal is very reminiscent of Maharaja: The Game of Palace Building in India. They both share theme and some mechanisms.

The rules are very easy to grasp, you can explain the game under 10 minutes and the playtime is around 60 minutes (with fast players). The game components are good and they look gorgeous on the map. The art is ok.

The theme is also ok, I don’t like it nor dislike it. It feels thematic for a Knizia game but in reality the theme is rather thin.

The luck of the draw exists but I feel it can be mitigated with clever play, you (almost) always can win at something, you can pass first to grab better cards to fight another day or you can skip the current turn to draw even more cards.

Taj Mahal features interesting decisions (namely which prize you will pursue in each region, but these decisions have far reaching consequences) and the gameplay is very tense and fun. You have to have the majority in the faction you want to score, and each faction gives you different bonuses and VPs. It also has an spatial element because where you build your palaces matters a lot. To summarize, the game has a lot of depth for such a simple game.

Bottom line, Taj Mahal is an ok game I would like to play from time to time. I already sold my copy of the game BUT this was done because I’m in a contracting game phase and I already have Maharaja: The Game of Palace Building in India, a game I like more. If requested I will play happily. One of the best Knizia´s games I have played.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: 7
I've heard Taj Mahal praised as a masterpiece and bashed as glorified "Chicken." I found it to be neither. The mechanism at the core of the game does resemble chicken: you have a hand of cards in several colors, and each turn you choose a color and then play cards of that color until A.) you decide to pull out for a reward, B.) you are forced to pull out because you have no more cards of the appropriate color, or C.) all the other players have pulled out. Strategy is quite abstract but at least interestingly diversified: watch the public card-draws made by other players, charge-up power plays to collect particular tiles of cumulative value, pull out of the auction at the right time in order to gain certain advantages, secure placements on the board that create valuable chains. A good game, but a bit of a hodgepodge, and a thematic lightweight.

Update: I've been thinking about this one off and on since trying it for the first time, and while it seems like an interesting game in theory, I just don't have that much desire to bother with it at the moment. That might be me more than anything. There's a lot of "game" here, but I'm not sure I have the temperment or the patience to "get into it."

RATING: 7.5
I really enjoyed this, but wasn't expecting something that seemed so abstract. Your cards have different items. You are trying to put cards down with the most items to bid in six simultaneous auctions. It sounds confusing, but once you play a round it is clear. You try to win Taj Mahals to place on the board. If your Taj Mahals are connected between regions they score extra points. There are also some bonus items that you can win for more points.

---
Difficult to say something meaningful about this game ten years after its release other than it's a great game which has been proven by time. It's as close to a card game as a big box boardgame can be while the positional play on the board opens up dimensions not available to card games. Best of both worlds! Tadsch Mahal is one of the rare cases where good tactical play is as important as strategic perspective. All of this comes with relatively simple rules - a rarity for meatier Eurogames today.

RATING: 7
1/17/08: So far, the subject of my only [b][url=http://boardgamegeek.com/article/1399727#1399727]session report[/url][/b] on BGG. 

From my SR:

...It seems like this is not really an auction in the normal sense, but maybe more like a betting game like 5 card stud poker. After every round of cards you have to reassess your opponents' cards and your chances of winning, and then decide whether to add another one of your precious bidding cards to the pot in an effort to win something or to fold while you can...
...I'm not sure what to make of this game yet, but I would play this again.

RATING: 5.5
Played 4 times.  My opinion of this game has dropped steadily since the first game.  Initially, I really liked this game.  It's sort of a pseudo-auction, and that means I should stink at it.  Even the loser of a battle could get something in the end though.  After each game, I was more annoyed, for non-obvious reasons.  I think I just never quite grasp when and where to pull the trigger in this game, and it ALWAYS seems that the other players are winning points almost randomly.  Points are won sort of majority control style.  It seems that the winners are those who win the most areas by spending the least resources BUT why the spend the least resources seems more like luck and less like strategy.  I'd be ok not playing this game again.

RATING: 6
For me, Taj seems solid with 4 players, and 3 too.  I really dislike playing this with five b/c the disparity between first and last place is so wide virtually every time I've played it 5-handed.  This may some times happens even with 4.  By mid-game, it's virtually impossible that the laggards of a 5-plyr game will even come close to competing - either through a falling behind problem or runaway leader issue.  With 3 or 4, there is more chance of being able to catch-up and come from behind.

RATING: 10
From what I understand, the game is ONLY good with four.  One seeks to gain points in various ways from taj mahal route connections, scoring different tiles of different products earned by card play which also accumulate over time, and through special cards and chits.  All these are won via vicious card play in which you can generally only play cards of one color.  You have got to know when to hold them AND when to fold them in this game as losing cards for nothing will really set you back in this game.  When its your turn, if you have the majority in either elephants, any of the four princesses, or the throne guy, you can exit card play with a score - otherwise, you must decide if you want to lay another card to contest a princess or elephant or fold.

RATING: 6
Témájában, külalakjában és stílusában hasonlít a Maharajához, mégis jelentős különbségek vannak a két játék között, a kézben lévő "haderő" fokozatos kijátszása (licitálás) pedig a Condottiere-re emlékeztet.

A játékot kicsit száraznak éreztem, de be kell látni, elég jól kitalált szabályai vannak, amiket ráadásul egyetlen játék alatt rendesen el lehet sajátítani. Szóval könnyen tanulható és teljesen nyelvfüggetlen játékról van szó. A játék 13 fordulóból áll, minden fordulóban a 13 tartomány valamelyikében folyik a küzdelem. A tartományokban legfeljebb 5-5 palotát lehet felépíteni 4-4 előre meghatározott - utakkal összekötött - helyen (egy helyre akár két palota is felhúzható). A cél az, hogy a számunkra legmegfelelőbb helyen/helyeken sikerüljön palotát építenünk, mert ezért pontokat vagy kártyákat kapunk, vagy esetleg a négy termék egyikét. Minél több palotámat sikerül úgy felépítenem, hogy egy útvonalra essenek, annál több pont jár érte. Minél többet összegyűjtök az adott termékből, az is annál több pontot ér.
Palotát az építhet, aki küzdelmet nyer az adott tartományban. A kezünkben lévő lapokon öt különböző szimbólum szerepelhet, és egymás után vagy kirak ezek közül egyet az adott játékos, vagy passzol. Passzolás esetén megnézzük, hogy hány színből van neki a legtöbbje kirakva épp, és annyi palotát felépít az adott tartományban (0-5 db-ot). Utána az adott tartomány esetén a passzoló játékos már nem küzdhet, a többi játékos viszont a maradék színekért építhető palotákért igen. A végén mindenki kap/választ néhány lapot a kezében lévők pótlására. Persze vannak ehhez még egyéb tudnivalók is, nem ilyen egyszerű ez, de könnyen elsajátítható és nagyon jól kitalált rendszerről van szó.

Folyamatos döntési helyzetet jelent, hogy lerakjak-e a kezemből lapokat küzdve a különböző színekért, vagy inkább maradjak ki az adott csatározásból és újabb lapokat felhúzva erősítsem magam, hogy a következő tartománynál már ne lehessen senki, aki legyőzhessen. Ha küzdök, a többiek kijátszott haderejét és a sajátomat, valamint a kezemben lévő lapokat összevetve el kell döntenem, hogy meddig folytatom a küzdelmet, és mikor szállok ki. Küzdök-e több színért is sok haderőt feláldozva, vagy megelégszem kevesebbel.
Nyert csata után döntési helyzet, hogy melyik csomópontokban építsem fel a palotámat. Nem egyszerű ezt eldönteni, mert pl. "A" helyen felépítve sok palotám válik úttal összekötötté, "B" helyen felépítve plusz pontot kapok, "C" helyen felépítve plusz lapot/terméket kapok, "D" helyen felépítve pedig keresztbe teszek a többi játékosnak, akiknek pont az hiányzik sok palotájuk összekötéséhez.

Szóval kellően összetett (de nem túlbonyolított!), gondolkodós, és kicsit blöffölős játék, tetszik. Tetszik az is, hogy lehet látni előre, hogy mik lesznek a következő tartományok, azokból mit nyerhetek, és annak függvényében, hogy az mennyire fontos nekem dönthetem el, hogy milyen elszántan kell majd küzdenem érte. Nem tetszik, hogy a már lerendezett tartományok nem igazán játszanak szerepet a későbbiekben, egyedül abból a szempontból, hogy hogyan vezetnek az utak és kiknek van az adott csomópontokban palotájuk.

A játék - bár analitikus gondolkodású agyhoz illik, mint pl a Tikal is - nem igényel sok számolgatást: ötnél tovább nem nagyon kell benne elszámolni, azt is csak a palotaépítéskori pontszámításnál. A távolabbi jövővel kapcsolatban is inkább az érzésekre lehet csak hagyatkozni, előre nem kiszámolható, hogy mi lenne a legjobb lépésem. Ez előny, hiszen így nem tölt el senki hosszú perceket azzal, hogy kiszámolja a különböző döntési lehetőségei várható végeredményét, mint pl. a Tikalban, nem lassul le a játék. Hátrány, hogy minden tartomány lezárásakor a húzópakliból ki kell rakni egymás mellé a hét legfelső lapot, ami ugyan 20 másodperc csak, de akkor is holtidő.

Szívesen játszanék vele máskor is, de nem érzem azt, hogy most rögtön mennem kell a boltba és meg kell vennem.

RATING: 10
While Taj Mahal’s reprint a couple of years ago was hotly anticipated, it did not seem to produce the acclaim it deserved. Before the reprint, it was a holy grail for board game collectors because it is a candidate for Reiner Knizia’s best design. It is definitely one of the most distinctive eurogames I’ve ever played.

First, it is the only game with randomized setup that is strategic because it lays the entire landscape of play out at the start. Right away, you know what you are fighting for and when. The only unknowns are the cards you use to bid, and an observant player will have a good idea about them, as well, after a couple of rounds.

Second, the way the auctions are evaluated whenever a player withdraws is ingenious. It creates a delicious tension between conserving resources and not wanting to leave openings for your opponents to drive through and seize, freezing you out of a key objective. Added to this is the tension between dropping quickly to seize a choice palace location and sticking things out to try winning more.

Plenty of euros, especially those with an area control element, feature tough choices based around choosing your battles so you don’t waste scarce resources, but no game does a better job than Taj Mahal of tempting you to commit just a little bit more. Often you are not only cutting loose sunk costs, you are genuinely tempted by unexpected opportunities, only to be defeated at the last second and left bereft. While the failures can be crushing, the tension is delicious and the victories all the sweeter because of the high stakes. Layered on top of this is the irony that moderation will get you further in the long run.

Tak Mahal is not just another auction game. The rhythm and the decisions are unique – it hasn’t even had many second-rate imitators – and a pleasure. Every eurogamer owes it to himself to try it out. It’s one of my top ten boardgames of all time and a masterclass in game design.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Didn't enjoy this much. Trying to analyze why:

1) No sense of progression: what you do in round 12 is pretty much the same as round 1. There's of course a bit more palace inter-connection and resource values differ per person, but the math is similar, the feel is similar, and the game just feels repetitive. Each bidding round is also the same decision multiple times (i.e. What percent do I win now if I quit vs if I continue? Do I care that much about this round or I should focus on next?).

2) Virtual elimination: The last 2 rounds were just me and one other person vying for first (we ended up tying score-wise), and 2 of the 5 players were out around round 8 or so. Having absolutely no chance of winning but still having to play another 30-45 minutes seems pretty bad... and their decisions became more and more arbitrary later on (which affected how the rest of us did).

3) The special 4 cards seemed rather over-powered; for instance, getting 2 bonus points every turn. In fact, the person who tied got nearly 20% of their points from the one special card (yellow). We all re-checked the rules since this seemed so bad halfway in...

4) Runaway leaders: Similar to comment #2. Basically if you're ahead, every item is worth more to you (e.g. gold/tea/etc, special cards compound to make you win more, etc). Just keeps amplifying your lead.

5) Somewhat arbitrary failure: One player's last card had him in the lead on 3 categories with 3 people left, and I had to back out immediately after; the other player played a card which tied him on all 3, so he got nothing (no more cards left). After that he was out of game (finished 2nd last), but I couldn't really fault his play... just very bad luck. Made the game seem more arbitrary to me.

6) Very minor, but I feel the player colors need to differ more.

On the pros side, I like the idea of inter-connection with previous decisions (though in practice most I and others got was 3; people would block and you needed to win two of four or crown), and the crown sharing a space was good. I think scoring only if you get resource or elephant means most players don't score most rounds, which led to wide disparities in score (first place had ~60 points, last place didn't get to 20).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
An elegant, big, Knizia bidding and placement game with a few different paths to victory.  You can win with a placement strategy (placing palaces on the board that connect to palaces in other regions) or with an ecomomic strategy (earning commodoties who's values are aggrandized, the more you own).  Or perhaps you could try going for an equal parts placement and economics strategy but you'll probably need to play more defense against your opponents.  The card play mechanism is very simple but makes for some very tough decisions (increase your influence which ultimately will weaken your hand for further turns or get out early).  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Played once.  This is a highly interactive game where players bid against each other for influence over each of twelve regions.  The bidding system is very neat: players place one or two cards that influence one of the six things in each city that can be bid on.  Players continue bidding in turn order until they withdraw, at which point they lose all the cards they play, choose two cards from the refresh area, and compare the amount of influence they have in each area in the city.  If, at the time they withdraw, a player has the most influence for any of the six categories, they win that category.  I love this bidding system, as it rewards tactical playing and prevents the bidding from just becoming a "who can stay in the longest" game (especially because the last person left in the auction only gets one card, whereas everyone else gets two).  

This game is all about the card management, and is very intereactive (as your bids must necessarily take into account everyone else's).  And, in true Knizia style, there are lots of ways to earn points (e.g., in my first game I won the game without winning a single province token).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played twice now....ok this game is hard to wrap your head around first time for sure but once everyone gets it after 3 or so rounds start over and play the game.  I thought Samurai scoring was difficult to explain...this one is a humdinger for sure but great bidding system I love it.  Most likely will go up after some more plays.  I'm guessing 4 or 5 is best though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It took a while for Taj Mahal to grow on me, but at this point it very well could be my favorite middle-weight board game. Although initial plays will run into the 90 minute range, I think you can keep it closer to 60, where there are few other competitive board games of this weight, especially for more than two players. Hansa Teutonica would probably be everyone else's choice; in fact, when folks were falling in love with Hansa Teutonica, I played this for the first time and found it to be the better game. Both have a nice mix of mechanisms, many ways to score, and a relatively quick playing time. But while Hansa Teutonica (like most other popular Euros) responds well to mostly tactical, short-term thinking, Taj Mahal requires players to think strategically. Your plans might not work out, but if you just go with the flow then you won't win very often. All in all, Taj Mahal squeezes a lot of game into 60 (to 90) minutes. A very satisfying, if occasionally frustrating, experience.

RATING: 10
A brilliant game that cleverly merges all the great Knizia mechanisms into a fascinating and tense whole. The best part is the hand management and how it works with the bidding for each region's rewards. The special cards, especially the Princess, add an interesting twist. Each game is very different, too. The way the map mechanism of scoring connected regions is very clever, too.

Only a couple of cons: 
- best played with 4 players only because there needs to be even competition in both the elephants and connected cities strategies. 3 and 5 players will usually let one of the strategies break out and run away.
- the map and general scheme colouring is drab and difficult to read. The graphic artist could have made so much of the many colours available from Indian culture and history.
- I love the game but I suck at it! I need to be more aggressive when bidding for the Princess.

RATING: 5
This one started off fun enough. I like the game of chicken that revolves around playing cards of a color, and trying to cash in when you have the most of a symbol. The problem is, the game goes on entirely too long for what it is. This game should be at most half as long as it is, as it becomes very repetitive after awhile. I think the decision space is interesting, and the bidding that goes on feels very unique. I also really like the route-building aspect of placing palaces, but the game just feels like kind of a slog at this point. I would much rather play something like Ra or Medici that packs the same tenseness in a much shorter time frame.

Playing this reminded me a lot of Rialto, but whereas that one was almost too bare-bones, Taj Mahal feels like a better design. It just needs to be as short as Rialto.

RATING: 10
Taj Mahal combines bidding and area majority and spice it with various set collection mechanisms to create a rich but hot stew. The core of the game is simply to fight over regions and score points by a) connecting previously placed palaces, b) earning powerful followers or c) collecting sets of similar goods. So far, nothing new.

But what really sets Taj Mahal apart is the bidding system where you take turns to play cards with values in six different categories. As soon as you pass, you claim the rewards for the categories you have a majority in but you also withdraw from the other categories, which may give your opponents opportunities. So do you accept a small reward to save cards for future regions or do you go all in for the big reward, potentially ruin your hand for the next round? The Indian cuisine is nothing for the faint-hearted!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Played 5/26/2007 (4 players, less than 2 hours).

RATING: 7
Taj Mahal is a design that I have a lot of respect for, and as a game it lives up pretty well to that respect.  I am terrible at the game, but I still enjoy the different paths to victory and the different elements that work well together.  To me, Taj Mahal feels like the final evolution of Knizia's other auction games.

It could be because I am bad at this one, but it seems like the players don't have enough control over what they win in a province to really make a strategy around winning certain things. I believe that I am probably in the minority in this, but that is how I feel.  The biggest knock I have against Taj Mahal, however, is that it just doesn't grab me.  I don't need a strong theme, but either the theme or the mechanics need to draw me in, and neither do. It just feels dry.  It's a good game, and it deserves more play, but there are other games I find to be more fun.

RATING: 8.5
In this Knizia game, play cards in a visit to Indian provinces to win influence in 5 different things. You win those items if you have majority of those tokens on your played cards or if everyone else drops out. Very tough decisions to make on each visit on whether to battle the other opponents or to withdraw. Different paths to victory make it playable again and again. My only criticism would be that the game can be quite confrontational in one player matching another player symbol for symbol (eg the last place player does this to another player). Plays best with 4 players; we had some bad experiences trying to play with 6 players with the white pieces and 5 players is also quite crowded.

RATING: 7
The auction in Taj Mahal is weird, but interesting.  You have to throw the same color of card every round, and depending on where you stand in each auction you get to place your pieces on the place of your choice.  Some auctions have goods and some give special abilities.  The game is quite interesting in how it decides who places where and it takes awhile to wrap your head around.  You will score points at the end of the game for connected networks, amount of goods you have, and perhaps something else?  It was awhile since I played this. 

I would like to play this game again, but I found it quite tough to pick a strategy and make it work throughout the game.  I enjoy it, but my players will scratch their heads at this one I think.
