RATING: 6
This horrendous graphical design (both cover and components) hides a very good abstract game. The mechanics connect naturally to create a game deeper than the standard Dr. Knizia's.

My issue it's the lack of control, especially with many players. There's no way you can "secure" an action and you will likely lose some important bets just by bad luck, not because your rivals are actively trying to deny you. That makes the temple game less profitable.

I personally don't like such a whimsical nature in 90 minutes games with this level of depth, but there are people who will gladly forgive this to enjoy the tension it creates.

In any case, no doubt it's one of the best Knizia's.

RATING: 4
[b]Taj Mahal[/b]

[i]This game fell completely flat for me. The different actions in the game did not coalesce into a fun gaming experience. The set collection is very basic and underwhelming, the route building served as a means to an end, but the bidding was somewhat interesting although it felt clunky. 

I think that sums up my feelings on this game perfectly, Taj Mahal is a clunky and counter intuitive game to play. Where as I feel that Medici, Modern Art and Ra all offer elegant and simple rules, my play of Taj Mahal felt like a slog. The previous 3 games can be taught in a matter of minutes and even if you don't play well it's more of a lack of understanding of the perceived value of the various items you are collecting; the actual bidding/auction mechanics are clean and intuitive. I can't say the same for Taj Mahal, and there is really nothing there that is calling me back to play it again (especially since I could be playing Modern Art, Ra or Medici).[/i]

RATING: 7
Hmmmm...  One 3-player game.  Immediate comparison was Maharaja which we also rented and tried recently, but I liked this one slightly better.  I can see from the comments that the calculating folk hate the chaos and chances to get smacked down on a bid.  

I thought it was interesting as we got more brutal, matching each others' bids as the game went on.  We had some back and forth leads as the game progressed.  3 players felt like I could look at the cards available, drop out early, and hoard enough to be sure of at least winning something.  I made the come back victory over the last 4 or 5 provinces to be scored.  

I think it is possible to use some strategy within the constraints of the cards you get.  Is there risk that someone else will outbid you despite your plans?  Yes, but the game would be kind of bland and samey without the chance of failure.  

I'm still not sure if it would get too samey with repeated plays, and I can see that 5-player would be much more unforgiving than 3.  I think I would like to try 4 and keep it to 3 or 4.  In an unlimited game world, I would buy this.  Right now, I'm moving it down my wishlist because there are just other games I would rather use my time and money on right now.

I really liked the subtle background art, but I think the provinces could have been slightly better delineated--a dark line for the borders.

RATING: N/A
規則:
*放置圖版，數值12的省份標記放在agra(5城省份)，其他隨機放置在每個省份一個，+4分版塊放在agra，其他方形獎勵版塊洗勻隨機放在紫色城市上，人物版塊分類放好，右上每種放一個，金色黃冠也放這，四張特殊卡放一旁，兩個黑米寶一個放在省份數字1並打省分版塊放到右上區
*剩下卡牌洗勻每人發六張，隱藏資訊，開人數*2-1張，每人拿一個顏色皇宮，分數標記放0，隨機起始拿一個黑色米寶
*遊戲輪流進行，玩家行動二選一
1.出一或兩張卡 選一種顏色出，自己之後同輪要出都要出此顏色，此外可以額外出一張白卡或特殊卡
2.撤退 不在出牌，並比較目前出的牌牌面結算，然後棄掉所有出的牌，特殊卡回手，然後從開牌區選兩張，不補，最後一位抽剩下的一張
>如果國王外的有一種人比所有其他人多則拿左上的對應人物標記，並放一個城堡到此省份的一個城市(放獎勵標記就拿獎勵)
>如果國王較多拿金色黃冠放到一個自己的皇宮上，然後把皇宮放在此省份一個城市上，可跟沒皇冠城市共存，不能拿獎勵
>大象比較多，拿走該省份版塊
*獲得物品分數為目前這個加上之前的所有各數的分數，一次拿兩個一個一個分開結算
*皇宮分數為不管該省份這次蓋幾個都一分加上相連皇宮通過的省份數的分數
*如果第一輪就跳過會額外抽一張頂牌補償
*當所有玩家都跳過時，有兩個一樣的角色版塊玩家返還板塊並拿對應的特殊卡(在別人那就搶過來)，之後起始左傳，黑色米寶到下個數字區，下個省份版塊移到右上(之前的移除)，補人物或皇冠標記放回，牌庫在開人數*2-1張
*遊戲在第12個省份結算完結束，每張特殊跟白卡一分，跟他顏色最多的獲得對應分數

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
One of the early games from Reiner Knizia which established him as a legend in game design. And I've really fallen in love with his games; they're uncomplicated, efficient, intuitive and fun. Perfect, really. And Taj Mahal is no exception. It's an auction game with network/route building, hand management, and set collection that works with several different strategies. If you're feeling lucky, you can play in every one of the twelve rounds and you might win something; it doesn't always pay out, but that's the risk. Smart, determined players who don't mind sitting out for some rounds can save cards and dominate in a given region, linking their network for more points and taking bonus cards on the way. I thought that Ra has perfected and distilled the auction mechanic down to its essence: pass or play. But Taj Mahal has a tension and thrill that isn't present in Ra. It's a very unique twist on auction games and a joy to play. I love seeing the face of a player who thought they were winning, only to realize that they wasted their cards to not gain anything during a turn. That may sound punishing but I promise it's more enjoyable than frustrating. I can't wait to play it again!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

A friend brought their copy around. A great game. Definitely on my 'I want one!' list.

RATING: 9

You must stay on your toes though as mistakes can be costly.

RATING: 7
I'm a Knizia fan, but have never heard of this one. Yet, I think it deserves a place in his top 20, maybe even at the bottom of his top 10. 

Likeable game with a strange combination of gameplay: 1) an auction mechanic based on the concept that the first to bail out with the most objects wins the object's bonus 2) placement of buildings to create chains  3) set collection of goods, 4) bonus points/cards strewed around the board and ready to pick, and 5) end scoring bonuses.

It seemed complicated, but we all understood the rules in about 15 minutes. Although, it's a little finicky (compared to Knizia's usually elegant rules) in terms of small little rules you can forget. It has a few rough spots, but that doesn't change the quality of the gameplay itself. 

Theme-wise, it is banal. But the gameplay can be tons of fun like Knizia's best. The central mechanic of the auction is classic Knizia:  simple but full of strategy. You change your tactics continually through the game since your card hand will change continually. The randomized board gets some good replayability. 

One complaint is that the components are lacking. The board is lackluster and the card artwork can often be confusing and hard to spot across the table. I'd like to see a makeover of this.

RATING: 5.75
A games that only makes sense once you start playing it and even then probably not earlier halfway through. The game is essentially a card-game with some bluffing and hand management beefed up with some placement and tile collection (yes, it is a Knizia). However, in this case, I found that there is less skill involved than in many other Knizia games and a lot comes down to luck in this game (That is, if you consider random actions by other players luck). It might be something you are used to when playing a Knizia game, but this one is particularly ugly and the 'theme' or visuals add nothing to the enjoyment of the game, let alone making easier to play. The cards are really confusing. Why show two elephants on the card when it is worth one ? I thought this was okay. A typical Knizia that is missing that certain something that most of his other games have.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I first played Taj Mahal when it was out of print and after just getting into Euro games in the early aughts.  The way a dehydrated man loves water, I loved Taj Mahal.  It's a game I always come back to and, in my opinion, is the best bidding game Knizia ever did.

I like being able to fight both strategic and tactical elements in the board games I play.  Taj Mahal has both and yet the two elements feel distinctly separate from each other.

The game constists of twelve rounds, one for each of 12 abstract regions ostensibly in India.  The only named area on the board is Agra with the Taj Mahal.  Each region has items players can pick up, things like tea, rice and jewels.  Players will also place palaces on a network of roads that connect the various regions.  Here's where the strategic part of the game comes from.  Goods provide points when you get them and additional points everytime you get another good of that type.  And palaces provide points for when placed, but they provide additional points when connected by road to palaces from other regions.  SO from the outset of the game you want to plan for where your palaces will go and what goods you are going to pick up.

Because this is a Knizia game, you are going to have to bid to get these things and this is where the game becomes viciously tactical.  Players collect cards throughout the game and are simultaneously bidding against each other for six different opprotunities to place palaces and get goods.  If you have the high bid on any one of the six when your turn comes around you can drop out and claim your goods and build palaces.  If not, you can still bow out or you can play another card and try to stay in the auction.  It's a big game of chicken because cards are currency and if you spend too much you hurt yourself for future auctions.  If you spend too little you don't win what you need to.  It is entirely possible to blow your whole wad and get nothing.

There are going to be key battles that each player would very much like to win.  And then the game can get competitive.  If a player is really on the ball they can time their bids for when no one else has cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Only played once. I don't like auction games very much so this game from the start did not have a good chance of getting a good rating from me.

What annoyed me during my first playing is that I played passively; I focused on winning auctions that no one cared about. The competition was so fierce for the elephants that I was able to monopolize on the princess for a very long time. I was also able to get quite a long chain of palaces built without much planning on my own.

To be fair, I think this was the first playing for almost everyone, and because it's an auction game with dramatically different ways to score points, no one could have pursued a decent strategy.

RATING: 7.5
Clever tense bidding system competing in multiple scoring arenas.  This is certainly a game of confrontation, but it really it seems to me like a game of deal-making: you offer the other player a way out with face (take the orange leader and a palace and an extra card) and I'll have blah blah.  Inherent in the offer you make is the understanding that you both risk your game position if you can't quickly make a deal.   The game does seem a bit brittle, so that if the situation is mis-read and two players refuse to make an early deal, they can take each other out of the game.  I am not convinced that this is a flaw despite having been burned by it recently (7 cards for not ONE thing!) as it puts the pressure on to find a solution.  I like it, it plays in a reasonable time, and gets the heart racing.  The negatives are a spurious theme, a bit of grind in the calculations, the potential to be badly hurt by someone else's bad play, and the fact that is seems to polarize people with some strongly disliking the evil tension of the bidding mechanic.

RATING: 10
http://www.ronkkonen.com/boardgames.html#Mahal
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tentative rating.  i just played it for the 1st time last week and was more or less pludding along because there was so much going on.  I have no certain opinion of how much I like the game; only a good deal of curiosity.

Online, i've heard the game being described as being a big game of poker or chicken, but i don't think either description is really accurate. It seems that the gut instinct approach to the game is to make big long-term plans, but the reality is that you need to cut your losses very frequently and not be Captain Ahab. Greed is the emotion that is constantly being checked.

I think 'nuclear arms race' is a better analogy than 'chicken'. Chicken is a game where you get bragging rights for having no fear. Even if you end up in a bloody tangle at the end, there's a certain pride you can take in saying, 'dear lord, those handlebars hurt!!'

In contrast, nobody wants to go down the unpleasant route of thermonuclear warfare. you hope your opponents will be rational and understand that you have basic, urgent needs to be fulfilled. You wish your opponents could see how kind you are at heart and that you wouldn't use the threat of using nukes if the situation wasn't so desperate. Mangling your opponents is the furthest thing from your mind; you are only using the tools of persuasion available to you.
That's what taj mahal feels like. It's greed in the same sense that thirsty people will glut themselves at a drinking fountain, and not the way jack welch amasses wealth through GE.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Fucking brutal. And don't listen to anybody who tells you it's silliness, or unstrategic, or chicken on steroids. Demanding, constant strategic vision, many-turn planning, hand management, bluffing, outguessing, and outpsyching your opponent required. 

UPDATE:
The more I play, the more I love this game. It sort of asks that you ride the breaking edge of chaos, tracking way too much data about who's trying for what and who pulled what cards. It demands that you make a sweeping plan, and then subjects that plan to every humiliation and depradation in the book.

I want to play, I want to ravage, I want to destroy.

RATING: 7
Tricky system of bluffing and knowing when to fold. If you want to memorise cards you can take it to another level. It's grown on me after a few plays - enough to want to keep playing it anyway.

It's a pick your battles game where you have some idea where you'll be in with a shot at winning but observing which cards the other players have been picking up. There's a very good card selection mechanic here where the first to pull out of a battle gets first choice of the cards on offer for future rounds. Add to that the strategic scoring goals laid out on the board from the beginning of the game, the character bonuses, and what you get is a very good game where the sum of its parts come together nicely. Losing a battle you've invested heavily in, or even winning an expensive battle, can be rough, but that's the fun and challenge in the game that separates it from your usual light and forgiving German board game. I like how it's unforgiving, and if things aren't going well then it's over in about an hour anyway.

RATING: 2.5
Played one 5 player game. I've won. 

Initial impression: 
I know what I did in order to win: Do the most stuff of the same kind from randomised matrix -> get points. Maybe it's also that I don't really get bidding with cards as opposed to open auction or blind bidding (through it's clever the way it's used here). Opaque, counter-intuitive and absent of any real life connotation. On the good side, I like the possibility to make middle range plans (3 turns deep), just enough to make it interesting, but not too brain melting. 

EDIT 1: Martin said it's a good game in here, so a solo play after. 

1. I understand why it's liked. The card play which combines bidding, bluffing and risk taking is the interesting part of it. Actually a very interesting part of it. I missed it as I haven't played it with good players who would make it tense.

2. Unfortunately there's no game connected to it. It's just set collecting matrix that gives VPs. And okay, this is why each person has different agenda in a bidding and what makes the double think interesting. I get all of these. 

3. It's ugly. I hate Power Grid design, but that one has a purpose and agenda behind it, this is just terrible. One of the ugliest games I've seen. 

4. It's themeless. Okay let me get this right: this is not an abstract, there's a certain push towards creating a theme and it fails on each and every count. Theme makes no sense when playing. There's a map: of nothing. There are provinces of nonexistance. Cities and fortresses that float in vacuum of half formed ideas. Compared to this Hansa Teutonica (which I consider an abstract) is ameritrash. Everything is too balanced and goes in groups of four so it makes no thematic sense, yet it's not even elegant as it's fiddly. Just a mess. Worse thing: spices. What the hell? What is that doing in this game? 

5. Games are an experience and this one fails in realising this. It's a machine that lacks surface. I don't think this can be an abstract (fiddly scoring) and it does attempt at making a theme - the attempt of a provincial amateur theatre with no budget and 19th century ideas. 

Conclusion: Somebody needs to do a very impressive thematic and visual overhaul to save the game from itself. [u]Just give me something my willing suspension of disbelief can be occupied with.[/u] I have to play it again with a proper group, but I can't see this going over 5 - it's a half finished work.

RATING: 8
Taj Mahal nicely combines area influence with bidding to make a great game, but doubts linger. In Taj, players use cards to bid for dominance in six different categories. This bidding, as many have pointed out, bears some small resemblance to poker: there are four basic colors/suits, there is bluffing, you fold when you no longer wish to or cannot continue, and your card play often "raises" the stakes. (To my thinking, the strongest point of connection is the bluffing.)

The game is played in 12 rounds, each one a battle for placing palaces and acquiring goods in the 12 different provinces of North Central India. Like many a Knizia game, there are different routes to making points (and winning), and you do best if you can balance them out. The six things you bid for are the roles of the princess, vizier, general, monk, mogul, and elephant. The first five give you a palace per role, winning the elephant gives you the goods in the province. Upon scoring, each good you attain earns you one point. Furthermore, you get points for matching goods from previous rounds. So goods are a potent source of points, and their effect is compounded as the game wears on. Also, you get to place one palace for each role you win that is not an elephant. No matter how many palaces you have in the province, they are worth a single point (total). But, if you manage to connect those palaces to palaces in other provinces, each province represented in the chain of palaces is worth a point. You don't get points per palace, but rather points per province connected by palaces. I don't know if this makes a lot of sense, but I want to highlight that there are a few different routes to making points.

The heart of Taj Mahal is the card play. You must make sure that you have enough cards to be competitive in bidding for the various roles, and sometimes it is worth it to skip the fight for a province completely, as this nets you a bonus card.

I have played Taj Mahal with 3 and 4 players. 3 players is great for learning the game, but every single 3 player game I have played in has had a massive runaway leader problem. The outcome of the game is obvious after playing through about 5/12 provinces. 4 players makes for a much tighter and interesting game, where it is really any 2 or 3 players at the end. My concerns are two-fold. First, a player who plays "incorrectly" will not only lose horribly, but will unintentionally benefit another player, perhaps catapulting her to a win over other players who are playing very well. So a learning game or two is necessary. All players must be reasonably competent in foiling others in the bidding process, or the system of checks and balances breaks down. My second concern is that, if all players are playing reasonably well, the win seems somewhat random. I'm not certain that long term strategy has much of a place in Taj Mahal. This leads to a competitive game where the winner feels determined by a die roll. I will say this, I like playing Taj Mahal. It is a fun experience. I'm just uncertain about its staying power right now. I suppose I need to play some more, and get some 5 player games in too. Among bidding games, I still prefer Ra to Taj Mahal, but I'll gladly play Taj any time.

RATING: 4
Taj Majal is a brilliant bidding/hand management game.  Really. Just because I am horrible at this and I can't win to save my life, has no bearing on how I rate it.  After I'd played this a few times I wanted to see what I was doing wrong, I kept trying to grog the game out.  So, I rated the game an 8 then because I wanted to keep playing.

Soon though, because of the harsh losing conditions I found the game more frustrating than fun. A misplay deals worse damage than you would expect, as you not only get nothing if you lose, but your one opportunity to get to a region is gone, along with that you also lose your cards and the privilege to get better cards.  

My real issue with the game play of this is that you can either memorize all the cards that people take or you can play and hope that your intuition pays off.  Most of the time it doesn't.  This is not a game for gut players, or at least not for gut players named Morgan Dontanville.

But, outside of my increasing desire not to play this, my only substantiated problem with the game (outside of the rules being horribly written) is that the theme is flimsy and the card design confuses people.

I can see why people love this game; I think that everyone should try this once to see if they like it.  It certainly is a unique experience that you aren't going to get anywhere else.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent auction/hand-management game with a nice blend of strategy and tactics. The gimmick is that players bid in auctions with cards showing 1-2 of six symbols; every auction is really six at once. If you pass while leading in any given category, you win its corresponding benefit immediately, which grants points through route-building and set-collection mechanisms. Timing of passing is important, so auctions are tense games of blocking (better play an elephant so Bob isn't leading when the bidding gets back to him!); better, auctions are everyone-pays, so you really don't want to be forced to pass if you aren't winning anything.

If there's a downside, it's that everyone-pays auctions can and do see players invest large amounts and come out with nothing. It's fine to say that knowing when to fight your battles is part of the strategy, but the randomly available cards can see multiple players unknowingly planning to fight hard in the same category for one juicy target or another, devastating the loser and doing little better for the winner. 

Still, a brilliant little game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The more I play it, the more overwhelmed by the luck of the draw and the less interesting it becomes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I see why others really like this game, but it's just so-so for me.  I'm discovering I'm not a huge fan of Knizia's games, and this is a classic Knizia game.  Sure the bits are fantastic and the rules are easy enough, but I think the game can have problems at the strategic level with certain groups of people.  I say this because there seems to be 2 fundamental strategies to this game; build lots of palaces to connect provinces or get lots of economic tiles.  If the majority of players tend to focus on one of these strategies it can really throw the game out of whack.  One might ask, then why don't those players switch strategies?  This isn't very feasible because the building strategy only works well if you focus on it from the start.  Switching to building mid-stream is not likely to be very successful.  Thus the game sort of forces you to stick with your initially chosen strategy.  I would pass on this game and play something like Blue Moon City for a similar game (conceptually speaking) but with much better execution.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[Alea 'Tadsch Mahal' 2000 edition] {India; bidding, hand-management, drafting connection, set collection; optimal 4-5p} Taj Mahal is a true gamers' game with multiple paths to victory. Like many Knizia's games, TM has only a thin theme and is built around a convoluted scoring system. Thus, TM is quite abstract and not intuitive. It's deep and multifaceted and is thus brain-teasing and unforgiving. Players have to consider many things: which and how many cards to play at each province, which power to pursuit, when to withdraw, where to place the palace, which province to influence, which bonus/influence tile to get, etc. The rule is clear and not complicated. One minor problem is that it doesn't flow smoothly. Counting score each time when a player withdraws disrupts the game significantly (and each scoring needs some work). 5p game is more competitive and exciting but the down time is stronger (scoring occurs 60 times!)

The "palaces" are beautiful but the illustrations of cards and tokens are just so-so. The bonus token is too small. It's clumsy to stack the oval influence tiles again and again.

RATING: 10
Darling you got to let me know ... Should I stay or should I go?

Such sweet pain. A simple series of 12 auctions, but in each auction you're bidding on 6 things simultaneously. And everything is valued differently by all players. And when it's your turn and you're winning a bid or two do you settle for those items or do you try to win more items but leave yourself open to losing what you could get now? And sometimes it's best to win something first rather than a bunch of stuff later. And don't run short of cards because that hurts like hell.

There's even an official 2-player variant that is simple and works well.

RATING: 5
One game with 5 players, all new to the game. Somehow didn't connect that much. As for myself: made some mistakes the first turns and was dead last the whole game due to lack of decent catch-up mechanism. 

This game suffers from the 'as good as its weakest player' syndrome: if everyone knows the game and is playing well, probably highly enjoyable. If people mess up they are immediately out of the game. As ganging up on the leader means about equal pain to yourself. Detrimental in a 3+ player game, and therefore nobody will do it. Meaning you could be screwing up in the first turn and having to sit out the rest of the game without any chance of catching up. And the game is pretty boring if you're not playing for the points.

Probably would rate this higher when played more and with a more experienced group. Takes some time to get warmed up to. Problem is: does it get that time with 200+ other games vying for attention? 

Will try one other time before passing final verdict though. See if its more enjoyable vying for the top position.

Math traded it in Essen Math trade 2012.

RATING: 9
It's like poker, with a board!  This game is definitely one of my favorite Knizia designs, although it has been difficult to get to the table (damn you, other games!).  The bluffing and second-guessing make for an intense 90 minutes, and everyone is constantly trying to thwart everyone else's plans.  You may have to cooperate with other players to prevent one person from running away with connections or resources, then watch as everyone turns on you the next round for the same reasons.  Good hand management is a definite must.  Four players is definitely this game's sweet spot.  Three players is okay, but five players makes the board painfully crowded.

RATING: 4
Kind of a crazy game, and ultimately not very fun. If somebody could get rid of a few of the mechanisms and turn this into about a 45-minute game it could be pretty good.

The cardplay is a good idea but a lot of the bluffing elements don't come through because a player can usually collect enough cards to garantee they'll win in at least one area.

The boardplay is just a mess. You can score points by placing your palaces onto the board in contiguous lines, placing directly on top of tiles that score points, collecting goods with the elephant symbols, and collecting goods by placing palaces. There are also special cards that move around from player to player. To be competetive it also seems like it would be mandatory to remember which cards your opponents have drafted, and that kind of memory element is not fun for me.

RATING: 9
Super awesome.  Could ultimately be my favorite board game.... need more plays to find out.  Really tough choices on weather to be confrontational or agreeable for each province.  Randomized set up.  More ways to score points than I care to list.  After being overwhelmed my first game, the game starts to reveal itself.  It uses poker style elements, at least in the sense one can play or fold.  One can play loose or tight, and it behooves you to counter the table. 

There is an immense amount of trackable information, but the game plays quite fluidly and lighter than the amount of information would suggest.  The perfect game design.  The game forces me to crawl into my opponents head.  Playing the players and game system in a perfect delight.  

 Taj Mahal is the game I was looking for, when I first started to search this site.  Thank you boardgamegeek and Reiner Knizia.

Also, Poker tells are relevant in this game.  If someone is strong they show the same body language as they will in poker when they are strong and also when they are weak.  My goodness, I can't speak highly enough of the enjoyment I get from each and every play.

People have a tendency to draw cards for the wrong reason to play.  If you have played and dislike it, try drawing more based on the rewards, instead of the suit type.  If you have 7 of the 8 yellow guys in the game, the most you'll need to play is 2.  The game of chicken that most people talk about I don't encounter.  The connectionists have 5 things to collect, while the elephant people have 1.  Beatiful self balancing.

RATING: 5
TRADED AWAY (7/31/08) --- Another highly regarded Knizia classic makes the list. I wanted to love Taj Mahal. Knizia plus Alea was a recipe for high expectations, which is why I gave Taj Mahal 8 plays before giving up on it and trading it away. I’ll start with the best thing that Taj Mahal has going for it, which is that it’s a rare eurogame that allows players to not only make short-term tactical decisions, but also long-term strategic decisions (i.e., whether to focus on connecting a network of palaces or on collecting goods such as rice and spices). Most eurogames are excessively tactical so Taj Mahal was a welcome reprieve from that tendency. However, the game just doesn’t work in my mind. Players are playing cards to attempt claim a few prizes available on each turn, with each prize going to the person who plays cards with the most symbols corresponding to that prize. The problem is that playing the cards goes around and around the table so an arms race can develop, and that everyone loses all of the cards played, regardless of whether they win any of the prizes. As a result, the players who happen to draw the cards and go after the uncontested prizes will win what they’re after easily, and the players who happen to get stuck in card battles will lose horribly. I’ve been on both ends of this and it’s really not enjoyable regardless of the result. For example, player A could get stuck over a few turns in card battles with B, C, and D, whereas B, C, and D are only stuck in those battles with A. Thus, A has 3 card battles, and everyone else has 1. The cards expended in one of these contests are valuable and both players in a battle lose in actuality, even the one who wins, because the one who technically wins really paid far too much for the prize usually, and the winner is the person who was fortunate enough to run off for a different prize cheaply. I know it’s supposed to be a game about picking your battles and essentially folding much of the time to avoid such costly contests, but you’re still wasting cards and coming up empty-handed when you fold and after repeated turns of folding you’re sure to find yourself lagging significantly. I’ll stick to the plenty of other Knizia games that work for me, and leave this to one the poker players.

Received as gift: Christmas 2006

RATING: 7
Edit:I've since played this game again and really loved it.  I'm leaving my previous comments for historical reasons.

[-]My play of this was so tainted I'm not sure I can give a real opinion on the game. We had fundamental rules completely wrong for half the game.[/-]

The way the game works is you play colored cards with symbols to your tableau (following the "suit" of the color you've started), and whenever it comes around to your turn you can withdraw, taking a bonus for any symbol you have more of than any other player (there are six types of symbols), building a palace for each bonus you took, and discarding all your played cards. 

There are special cards that can be played with your normal color cards, and some of the special cards are won by getting sets of bonus tiles, and those stay in your hand round to round. You draw 2 cards after you withdraw (or 1 for the last one out). There is a route building and set collection element.

[-]Some rules we had wrong: How you get palaces, how many palaces you can build a turn, how many bonus tiles are available to win, whether your cards go away when you withdraw, how you score for playing palaces, and endgame scoring. As we got deeper and deeper into the game it became apparent that something was wrong and we finally stopped to read through the rules and fixed it.

I did have a concern about a "rich getting richer" runaway leader problem... but it can probably be countered by dropping out of certain provinces, saving cards and judiciously choosing when and where you want to compete, but none of that was obvious to me on the first play, especially as we didn't understand the basics of how the game worked. So further plays would probably make this a better game for me. But after the bad taste my game experience left in my mouth, I don't really want to play this thing again.[/-]

After playing the game again, with the correct rules, the game was actually a very good game that I enjoyed.  It's a bit sad that my initial play was so horrible.  But that was the fault of a poor rules explanation (did we get any rule right?) and not a fault of the game at all.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
About [thing=475][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=475][/thing] is a strategy game focused in hand management, set collection and building routes. In it, players use a limited hand of cards in order to put castles on the board, take goods, points and cards, and/or collect the economical influence of the province.

While the game is easy to teach and has a truly simple set of options for the players (play cards or withdraw), it carries a weight in the decisions and some deep thought, not only about what is happening right now - which province is being disputed, what it has, what cards where already played, what cards are open for taking -, but also what will occur next - cards to keep for later disputes, what the next provinces have in their economy token, and so on.

The replay value is high: the set up is random, both for the position of the goods as well as the order of resolution of the provinces. The draw of cards, for the starting hand and the available for drawing, also have impact on the game.

Production value is fine: the castles are pretty neat, while the board, tokens and cards aren't pretty, but do their job.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
Players fight to influence provinces, one by one, be to take goods, gain cards or points (the three types of bonus tiles), add castles and/or receive the economic tiles of the province (all contain 2 goods, except the first province), as well as influence tiles.

In each turn the player has 2 options:
- Play one or two cards (one colored; one colored + one white; one colored + one special);
- Withdraw.

If playing, the first card used can be of any color, but, afterward, the next cards must be of the same color as the first one. The second card played must be a neutral (white) card or a special card. Therefpre, only one colored card can be played each turn - white and special cards can be played alone.

When the person withdraws, she compares the influence she has, in each of the six areas:
- Vizir, General, Monk and Princess: if the person has the highest influence in one or more of these, she takes the connected influence tile (for exemplo, the Vizir tile when winning the Vizir), then places a castle (in open places) for each area she won - if she gets one or more bonus tiles, she scores them immediately. She also gain 1 point for a castle added in the province (regardless of how many) and 1 extra point of each province the player can connect by an unbroken line of their own castles;
- Grand Mogul: the person puts a castle in any spot in the province, even if the place already contains a castle, and scores it;
- Elephant: the person with the most elephants wins the economic tile of the province, and scores it immediately.

After the player finish her withdraw, the cards are discarded, which can influence the withdraw of other players, since now one or more might now be winning areas of influence that they weren't before. Finally, the withdrawing player draws two cards from the available cards. The last person to withdraws only gains one card.

If a player accumulates two influence tiles of the same type, she takes the special card corresponded to it. Special cards can be lost, if another player later gets two of the influence you used to gain the special card - the advantage is that special cards, once used, aren't discard, returning to its owner hand. 

Once everyone withdrew, the visit is over. Play will proceed in the next province.

Play ends when all provinces are visited. Then player scores the cards left in their hands: one point for each white and special card; one point for each card of the color the player has the most.

The player with the most points will be the winner!

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
- Which card to play. This takes into consideration what is up for grabs in the province, the position of it (for scoring purposes of castles), the influence tiles the player has her eyes on (to gain a special card), the other cards in her hand (considering the next cards must follow the same color), etc;
- When withdrawing. Sometimes is clear when to do it: if you are winning two areas, with a single card, is likely good to withdraw. However, this isn't always the case, because isn't simple about what you will get, but what you will be opening for the others to get as well. Sure, you can get a +2 points bonus tile and a fine position for a castle, but if this will be at the cost of giving two influence tiles to the next player, plus the economic tile, will it be worth? After each withdraw, the balance of power shifts, not always for the best for you. On the other hand, what proceed in the visit will net you? Maybe you will only lose important cards, to get the same thing as right now - or, worst, not being able to carry on and lose everything, pushed to withdraw. This is the brilliance of [thing=475][/thing]: a simple action, but with a LOT to think about and to evaluate;
- Which cards to take: is normally best to take colors you already have, or neutral cards, as these usually allow for a quick win of an area. However, it might pay off to go for a symbol, in order to being able to gain the influence required to gain a special card;
- Where to place a castle: be to take a bonus tile and/or to position it to score more now or later.

Overall the decisions are clear, without much complexity, yet all of them have branching elements and can't be taken lightly, as the main two (play, withdraw) have many aspects to consider.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Easy to teach;
- Language independent;
- Great depth to rules ratio;
- High interaction, without being agressive;
- Game starts small and builds up.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- It has some random factors, in the display of cards and drawing (with bonus tiles). This is more true after the initial distribution of cards, but the designer balanced this by making the economic value of the first province worth one good, while all the others give two (as from the second round on you already get to draw cards from the display, making your own hand);
- Scoring of castles can lead to some mistakes;
- Best with 4 players.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Remembering a time where Reiner Knizia was the Grand Mogul, as [thing=475][/thing] is tense, tight, leading to some strong competition, several choices, with a lively pacing. All while avoiding mini-games and an overly complex overhead.

[thing=475][/thing] keeps true to many of the Knizia classics: short rules, few options, while also being able to bring an unexpected depth, many hard decisions, a good deal of interaction (not harmful) and much to think about on each turn. It has that great beauty in simplicity.

RATING: 8
I played this game twice at EuroQuest, and I liked it alot.I think turn order matters more in this game than any other game I have played.  Being late in the turn order lets you react to what others have played.  Going first lets stake an early claim and if needed to get out first.Each card battle can have a very different flavor since you sometimes need to gain a quick majority in one area and then get out fast and other times you need to be ready for a potracted battle.Having first or last crack at a region partially determines what regions are more important to you, and you will have to pick your battles carefully.  If the region isn't important, either grab what you can and get out, or just stay out of it completely and get an extra card.There are lots of things to choose from in this game.

RATING: 9
I'm not so hot at this game, but I enjoy it anyway.  It's brinksmanship at its best, where you are constantly trying to make the most out of a minimal number of card plays.  Keeping yourself flexible is very important.  This game is amazing that manages to make it SEEM like going first is an advantage, but such is not always the case.  Really beautiful bits, probably the best that I've seen in a Euro game.  The colors are beautiful, the art is fantastic, and the whole comes out with a wonderfully cohesive look.  Lots to think about, and nice and tense.  One of Knizia's best.  My preferred way is to play where the white cards you get from the nobles are one-shots, not where you are able to take them back into your hand every time.  I find that otherwise the emphasis tends to be too heavy on yellow at all times.

RATING: 9
The main reason I play games is to feel the thrill of agonizing, nail-biting tension, and no other game takes me there quite as effectively as Taj Mahal. The Euro equivalent to Poker without the tedium of waiting to get a playable hand. The game is decidedly strategic, but knowing when to cut your losses or otherwise adjust when your best laid plans are interrupted is integral to success. Not an intuitive game and because of its harshness and the "all-or-nothing" nature of the auctions perhaps not for everyone, but for me it is a bundle of bliss packaged in 90 mins. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It's hard to categorize this game, since it takes into account hand management principles, area control, and balancing your interests in certain commodities.  I like the blend of the mechanics, but the learning curve is pretty steep.  In my first game, I thought I was doing well for the first half of the game, and then watched everything swirl into oblivion.  In my second game, things started to click, and I was impressed with how balanced the game was, as well as with how varied my options were.  It's very important to know when to start, bail, or continue in a fight (and to hold onto cards in short suits for when you have to prevent another player from getting a lot of stuff for next to nothing), but it's also fun to trash talk the other players in a fight to see how many cards they can play from their hands.

5 plays: 5 players

RATING: 6.7
在濃濃的地方美術下，讓我第一眼認定為簡單的遊戲性，喔不!
我還真是不了解RK，這款泰姬瑪哈陵可說是經典的爆腦、計算遊戲之ㄧ。

遊戲分為兩條主要得分路線:貨物流(收集大象)和蓋房流。
(在遊戲快結束的時候，還有收集同色牌的小加分方式。
遊戲就是你知我懂的比多寡。
輕策性的手牌管理，因為加分的方式不同，讓策略性更為提升。
出牌跟退場的時機，是這遊戲的最大重點。
如果您是渴望獲勝的，還得不時去猜想對手的套路，詳記每回合對手拿取的牌色，更別提還加了一點佔地先後順序的特性...
得分的計算上，還是不忘賞了玩家ㄧ個耳光，貨物種類累積、地域性累積，在這直白但變化高深的方式下，強制讓玩家們互相碰撞。

規則簡單，美術跟遊戲帶入感不足，但完全不重要 XD
可是非常吃人腦CPU，也很吃玩家之間的互動走向。
如果遇到思考魔人，或是重策王者，這款遊戲策略度瞬間爆表啊!!!

RATING: 7
Not an easy game by any means.  I like the fact that you can't really have it "figure out" as there are different ways to attain VP.  Taj Mahal also encourages long term planning, and in that aspect it becomes fairly unforgiving if things do not go your way as the play progresses.  It seems that having to change your strategy on the fly is often a sure step to losing.

I don't really mind the set collection part nor the bluffing mechanic.  However, the play order often feels like it has too much significant impact to how each province is played out.  There is also the pain of the constant bookkeeping in the scoring as well as who has claims on each of the special cards.  Not to mention the repetitive nature of the rounds.

An interesting design to say the least.  This is a provisional rating for the moment, will require more exposure to actually nail down my enjoyment for the game.

Played a LOT of this one, lately.

RATING: 8
After 2+ Plays: Reminds me of poker--you have to know when to fight for the prize, and when to cut your loses and get out.  If you like that type of game I suggest you try [GAMEID=112], which has a similar feel but, IMO, is much more fun.

After 10+ Plays: I still like [GAMEID=112] more, but I've grown to appreciate Taj Mahal more as well.  There are 3 main ways to gain points: connections, elephants, and the princess card, and I really like how each strategy is subtly affected by the number and proclivities of other players. Bumped rating from 7 to 8.

Great mechanics. What makes this game in my opinion is the poker element. Knowing when to fold is crucial. I've only knocked this down a couple of points since the luck element prevents a long term strategy. Very replayable and very addictive.

Very good tipical Knizia geme. It's a poker flavoured game that each turn request a different approach. The bid mechenic is exciting and there are different ways to reach the victory. Overall a very interesting game concept.
